<p><b>Newton Leroy</b> "<b>Newt</b>" <b>Gingrich</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'n' in 'no'">n</span><span title="/uː/ long 'oo' in 'food'">uː</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span></span> <span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'g' in 'guy'">ɡ</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="/ŋɡ/ 'ng' in 'finger'">ŋɡ</span><span title="'r' in 'rye'">r</span><span title="/ɪ/ short 'i' in 'bid'">ɪ</span><span title="/tʃ/ 'ch' in 'china'">tʃ</span></span>/</a></span></span>; born <b>Newton Leroy McPherson</b>; June 17, 1943) is an American politician from Georgia and author who served as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Speakers_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="List of Speakers of the United States House of Representatives">50th</a> <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Speaker of the United States House of Representatives">Speaker of the United States House of Representatives</a> from 1995 to 1999. He represented <a href="/wiki/Georgia%27s_6th_congressional_district" title="Georgia's 6th congressional district">Georgia's 6th congressional district</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> from 1979 until his resignation in 1999. In 2012, Gingrich was a <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich_presidential_campaign,_2012" title="Newt Gingrich presidential campaign, 2012">candidate</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_2012" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 2012">Republican Party presidential nomination</a>.</p>
<p>In the 1970s, Gingrich taught history and geography at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_West_Georgia" title="University of West Georgia">University of West Georgia</a>. During this period he <a href="/wiki/Political_campaign" title="Political campaign">ran</a> twice (1974 and 1976)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">United States House of Representatives</a> before winning in <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1978" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1978">November 1978</a>. He served as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Party_whips_of_the_United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="Party whips of the United States House of Representatives">House Minority Whip</a> from 1989–95.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>A co-author and architect of the "<a href="/wiki/Contract_with_America" title="Contract with America">Contract with America</a>", Gingrich was a major leader in the Republican victory in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_election,_1994" title="United States House of Representatives election, 1994">1994 congressional election</a>. In 1995, <a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)"><i>Time</i></a> named him "<a href="/wiki/Time_Person_of_the_Year" title="Time Person of the Year">Man of the Year</a>" for "his role in ending the four-decades-long Democratic majority in the House".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr20111208_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-npr20111208-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>As House Speaker, Gingrich oversaw passage by the House of <a href="/wiki/Personal_Responsibility_and_Work_Opportunity_Act" title="Personal Responsibility and Work Opportunity Act">welfare reform</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Taxpayer_Relief_Act_of_1997" title="Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997">capital gains tax cut</a> in 1997. In 1998, the House passed the first balanced budget since 1969. The poor showing by Republicans in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1998" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1998">1998 Congressional elections</a>, a reprimand from the House for Gingrich's <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Representatives_expelled,_censured,_or_reprimanded#Reprimanded_Representatives" title="List of United States Representatives expelled, censured, or reprimanded">ethics violation</a>, and pressure from Republican colleagues, resulted in Gingrich's resignation from the speakership on November 6, 1998.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cnn981106_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-cnn981106-3">[3]</a></sup> He resigned altogether from the House on January 3, 1999.</p>
<p>Since leaving the House, Gingrich has remained active in public policy debates and worked as a political consultant. He founded and chaired several policy <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Think_tanks" title="Think tanks">think tanks</a>, including <a href="/wiki/American_Solutions_for_Winning_the_Future" title="American Solutions for Winning the Future">American Solutions for Winning the Future</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Health_Transformation" title="Center for Health Transformation">Center for Health Transformation</a>. He has written or co-authored 27 books. In May 2011, he announced his campaign for the <a href="/wiki/Newt_Gingrich_presidential_campaign,_2012" title="Newt Gingrich presidential campaign, 2012">Republican presidential nomination</a>. On May 2, 2012, Gingrich ended his presidential campaign and endorsed front runner <a href="/wiki/Mitt_Romney" title="Mitt Romney">Mitt Romney</a>, who won the nomination.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>After being raised <a href="/wiki/Lutheranism" title="Lutheranism">Lutheran</a> and spending most of his adult life as a <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist</a>, Gingrich converted to Roman Catholicism in 2009. He has been married three times, with the first two marriages ending in divorce after he had affairs with other women. He has two children from his first marriage and has been married to <a href="/wiki/Callista_Gingrich" title="Callista Gingrich">Callista Gingrich</a> (née Bisek) since 2000.</p>
<p>On July 12, 2016, Gingrich was reported to be on <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s three-person shortlist to be the presumptive Republican nominee's running mate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_2016" title="United States presidential election, 2016">2016 presidential election</a>, along with New Jersey Governor <a href="/wiki/Chris_Christie" title="Chris Christie">Chris Christie</a> and Indiana Governor <a href="/wiki/Mike_Pence" title="Mike Pence">Mike Pence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VP_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-VP-5">[5]</a></sup> On July 15, 2016, Trump officially announced that Pence would be his running mate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 17, 2016, Gingrich announced he would not be serving in <a href="/wiki/Donald_Trump" title="Donald Trump">Donald Trump</a>'s cabinet but will instead be focusing on strategic planning for the Republican party to build on their victories ahead of the 2018 mid-term elections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McClatchyDC_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-McClatchyDC-7">[7]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life.2C_family.2C_and_education">Early life, family, and education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life, family, and education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Newton Leroy McPherson was born at the <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg_Hospital" title="Harrisburg Hospital">Harrisburg Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg, Pennsylvania</a>, on June 17, 1943. His mother, Kathleen "Kit" (née Daugherty; 20 Nov 1925–2003), and father, Newton Searles McPherson (1923–1970),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> married in September 1942, when she was 16 and McPherson was 19. The marriage fell apart within days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> He is of German, Scottish, and Irish descent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1946, his mother married career Army officer Robert Gingrich (1925–1996), who adopted Newt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stepfather_obit_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-stepfather_obit-13">[13]</a></sup> Robert Gingrich was a career Army officer who served tours in Korea and Vietnam. In 1956 the family moved to Europe living for a period in <a href="/wiki/Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Orléans">Orléans</a>, France and <a href="/wiki/Stuttgart" title="Stuttgart">Stuttgart</a>, Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gingrich has three younger half-sisters, <a href="/wiki/Candace_Gingrich" title="Candace Gingrich">Candace and Susan Gingrich, and Roberta Brown</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stepfather_obit_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-stepfather_obit-13">[13]</a></sup> Gingrich was raised in <a href="/wiki/Hummelstown,_Pennsylvania" title="Hummelstown, Pennsylvania">Hummelstown</a> (near Harrisburg) and on military bases where his father was stationed. The family's religion was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lutheran" title="Lutheran">Lutheran</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> He also has a half-sister and half-brother, Randy McPherson, from his father's side. In 1960 during his junior year in high school, the family moved to Georgia at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Benning" title="Fort Benning">Fort Benning</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1961, Gingrich graduated from <a href="/wiki/Baker_High_School_(Columbus,_Georgia)" title="Baker High School (Columbus, Georgia)">Baker High School</a> in <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Georgia" title="Columbus, Georgia">Columbus, Georgia</a>. He had been interested in politics since his teen years while living with his family in <a href="/wiki/Orl%C3%A9ans" title="Orléans">Orléans</a>, France. He visited the site of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Verdun" title="Battle of Verdun">Battle of Verdun</a> and learned about the sacrifices made there and the importance of political leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Newt_Gingrich_as_a_young_professor_in_history.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1245" data-file-width="843" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Newt_Gingrich_as_a_young_professor_in_history.jpg/200px-Newt_Gingrich_as_a_young_professor_in_history.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Newt_Gingrich_as_a_young_professor_in_history.jpg/300px-Newt_Gingrich_as_a_young_professor_in_history.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Newt_Gingrich_as_a_young_professor_in_history.jpg/400px-Newt_Gingrich_as_a_young_professor_in_history.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Newt_Gingrich_as_a_young_professor_in_history.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Newt Gingrich as a young history professor</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gingrich received a B.A. degree in history from <a href="/wiki/Emory_University" title="Emory University">Emory University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a> in 1965. He went on to graduate study at <a href="/wiki/Tulane_University" title="Tulane University">Tulane University</a>, earning an <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Arts" title="Master of Arts">M.A.</a> (1968) and a Ph.D. in European history (1971).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> He spent six months in <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a> in 1969–70 working on his dissertation, <i>Belgian Education Policy in the Congo 1945–1960.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gingrich received <a href="/wiki/Selective_Service_System" title="Selective Service System">deferments</a> from the military during the years of the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> for being a student and a father, as he had married and had a child. In 1985, he stated, "Given everything I believe in, a large part of me thinks I should have gone over."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1970, Gingrich joined the history department at <a href="/wiki/University_of_West_Georgia" title="University of West Georgia">West Georgia College</a> as an assistant professor. In 1974 he moved to the geography department and was instrumental in establishing an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interdisciplinary" title="Interdisciplinary">interdisciplinary</a> <a href="/wiki/Environmental_studies" title="Environmental studies">environmental studies</a> program. Denied <a href="/wiki/Academic_tenure" title="Academic tenure">tenure</a>, he left the college in 1978 when he was elected to Congress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_political_career">Early political career</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early political career">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gingrich was the southern regional director for <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Rockefeller" title="Nelson Rockefeller">Nelson Rockefeller</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_presidential_primaries,_1968" title="Republican Party presidential primaries, 1968">1968 Republican primaries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Congressional_campaigns">Congressional campaigns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Congressional campaigns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1974" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1974">1974</a>, Gingrich made his first bid for political office as the Republican candidate in <a href="/wiki/Georgia%27s_6th_congressional_district" title="Georgia's 6th congressional district">Georgia's 6th congressional district</a>, which stretched from the southern <a href="/wiki/Atlanta" title="Atlanta">Atlanta</a> suburbs to the <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a> state line. He lost to 20-year incumbent Democrat <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_James_Flynt,_Jr." title="John James Flynt, Jr.">Jack Flynt</a> by 2,770 votes. Gingrich ran up huge margins in the suburban areas of the district, but was unable to overcome Flynt's lead in the more rural areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Gingrich's relative success surprised political analysts. Flynt had never faced a serious challenger; Gingrich was the second Republican to ever run against him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> He did well against Flynt although 1974 was a disastrous year for Republican candidates nationally due to fallout from the <a href="/wiki/Watergate_scandal" title="Watergate scandal">Watergate scandal</a> of the Nixon administration.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Gingrich sought a rematch against Flynt in 1976. While the Republicans did slightly better in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1976" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1976">1976 House elections</a> than in 1974 nationally, the Democratic candidate in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1976" title="United States presidential election, 1976">1976 presidential election</a> was former Governor of Georgia <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Carter" title="Jimmy Carter">Jimmy Carter</a>. Carter won more than two-thirds of the vote in his native Georgia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Gingrich lost his race by 5,100 votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Gingrich primed for another run in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1978" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1978">1978 elections</a>, Flynt decided to retire. Gingrich defeated Democratic State Senator Virginia Shapard by 7,500 votes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Gingrich was re-elected six times from this district.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> He faced a close general election race once—in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_House_election,_1990" title="United States House election, 1990">House elections of 1990</a>—when he won by 978 votes in a race against Democrat David Worley. Although the district was trending Republican at the national level, conservative Democrats continued to hold most local offices, as well as most of the area's seats in the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_General_Assembly" title="Georgia General Assembly">General Assembly</a>, well into the 1980s.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_Congress">In Congress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: In Congress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In 1981, Gingrich co-founded the Military Reform Caucus (MRC) and the Congressional Aviation and Space Caucus. During the <a href="/wiki/1983_congressional_page_sex_scandal" title="1983 congressional page sex scandal">1983 congressional page sex scandal</a>, Gingrich was among those calling for the <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_from_the_United_States_Congress" title="Expulsion from the United States Congress">expulsion</a> of representatives <a href="/wiki/Dan_Crane" title="Dan Crane">Dan Crane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gerry_Studds" title="Gerry Studds">Gerry Studds</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT2_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT2-29">[29]</a></sup> Gingrich supported a proposal to ban loans from the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a> to Communist countries and he endorsed a bill to make <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King,_Jr." title="Martin Luther King, Jr.">Martin Luther King, Jr.</a>'s birthday a national holiday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ronald_Reagan_with_Newt_Gingrich.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="492" data-file-width="750" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ronald_Reagan_with_Newt_Gingrich.jpg/240px-Ronald_Reagan_with_Newt_Gingrich.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ronald_Reagan_with_Newt_Gingrich.jpg/360px-Ronald_Reagan_with_Newt_Gingrich.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Ronald_Reagan_with_Newt_Gingrich.jpg/480px-Ronald_Reagan_with_Newt_Gingrich.jpg 2x" width="240"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ronald_Reagan_with_Newt_Gingrich.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Rep. Gingrich meets with President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>, 1985.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1983, Gingrich founded the <i>Conservative Opportunity Society</i> (COS), a group that included young conservative House Republicans. Early COS members included <a href="/wiki/Robert_Smith_Walker" title="Robert Smith Walker">Robert Smith Walker</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judd_Gregg" title="Judd Gregg">Judd Gregg</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dan_Coats" title="Dan Coats">Dan Coats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Connie_Mack_III" title="Connie Mack III">Connie Mack III</a>. The group gradually expanded to include several dozen representatives,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balz_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Balz-31">[31]</a></sup> who met each week to exchange and develop ideas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roberts_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gingrich's analysis of polls and public opinion identified the group's initial focus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balz_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-Balz-31">[31]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> adopted the "opportunity society" ideas for his 1984 re-election campaign, supporting the group's conservative goals on economic growth, education, crime, and social issues. He had not emphasized these during his first term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashPo84_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-WashPo84-32">[32]</a></sup> Reagan also referred to an "opportunity" society in the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" title="State of the Union address">State of the Union address</a> of his second term.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Balz_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-Balz-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>In May 1988, Gingrich (along with 77 other House members and <a href="/wiki/Common_Cause" title="Common Cause">Common Cause</a>) brought ethics charges against Democratic Speaker <a href="/wiki/Jim_Wright" title="Jim Wright">Jim Wright</a>, who was alleged to have used a book deal to circumvent campaign-finance laws and House ethics rules. During the investigation, it was reported that Gingrich had his own unusual book deal, for <i>Window of Opportunity,</i> in which publicity expenses were covered by a <a href="/wiki/Limited_partnership" title="Limited partnership">limited partnership</a>. It raised $105,000 from Republican political supporters to promote sales of Gingrich's book.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Gingrich's success in forcing Wright's resignation contributed to his rising influence in the Republican <a href="/wiki/Caucus" title="Caucus">caucus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 1989, Gingrich became <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_Minority_Whip" title="House Minority Whip">House Minority Whip</a> in a close election against <a href="/wiki/Edward_Rell_Madigan" title="Edward Rell Madigan">Edward Rell Madigan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Komarow_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Komarow-35">[35]</a></sup> This was Gingrich's first formal position of power within the Republican party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Drinkard_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Drinkard-36">[36]</a></sup> He said his intention to "build a much more aggressive, activist party".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Komarow_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Komarow-35">[35]</a></sup> Early in his role as Whip, in May 1989, Gingrich was involved in talks about the appointment of a <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panamanian</a> administrator of the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>, which was scheduled to occur in 1989 subject to U.S. government approval. Gingrich was outspoken in his opposition to giving <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torrijos-Carter_Treaties" title="Torrijos-Carter Treaties">control over the canal</a> to an administrator appointed by the dictatorship in <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gingrich and others in the House, including the newly minted <a href="/wiki/Gang_of_Seven" title="Gang of Seven">Gang of Seven</a>, railed against what they saw as ethical lapses during the nearly 40 years of Democratic control for almost 40 years. The <a href="/wiki/House_banking_scandal" title="House banking scandal">House banking scandal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Post_Office_scandal" title="Congressional Post Office scandal">Congressional Post Office scandal</a> were emblems of the exposed corruption. Gingrich himself was among members of the House who had engaged in <a href="/wiki/Check_kiting" title="Check kiting">check kiting</a>; he had <a href="/wiki/Overdraft" title="Overdraft">overdrafts</a> on twenty-two checks, including a $9,463 check to the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">Internal Revenue Service</a> in 1990.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:NewtGingrich.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="240" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/NewtGingrich.jpg/170px-NewtGingrich.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/NewtGingrich.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:NewtGingrich.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gingrich's official portrait as a Congressman</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1990, after consulting focus groups<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arsenal_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-arsenal-39">[39]</a></sup> with the help of pollster <a href="/wiki/Frank_Luntz" title="Frank Luntz">Frank Luntz</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/GOPAC" title="GOPAC">GOPAC</a> distributed a memo with a cover letter signed by Gingrich titled "Language, a Key Mechanism of Control", that encouraged Republicans to "speak like Newt." It contained lists of "contrasting words"—words with negative connotations such as "radical", "sick," and "traitors"—and "optimistic positive governing words" such as "opportunity", "courage", and "principled", that Gingrich recommended for use in describing Democrats and Republicans, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-arsenal_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-arsenal-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to population increases recorded in the 1990 United States Census, Georgia picked up an additional seat for the <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_elections,_1992" title="United States House of Representatives elections, 1992">1992 U.S. House elections</a>. However, the Democratic-controlled <a href="/wiki/Georgia_General_Assembly" title="Georgia General Assembly">Georgia General Assembly</a>, under the leadership of fiercely partisan Speaker of the House <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tom_Murphy_(U.S._politician)" title="Tom Murphy (U.S. politician)">Tom Murphy</a>, specifically targeted Gingrich, eliminating the district which he Gingrich represented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-41">[41]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Gerrymandering" title="Gerrymandering">Gerrymandering</a> split Gingrich's territory among three neighboring districts. Much of the southern portion of Gingrich's district, including his home in <a href="/wiki/Carrollton,_Georgia" title="Carrollton, Georgia">Carrollton</a>, was drawn into the <a href="/wiki/Columbus,_Georgia" title="Columbus, Georgia">Columbus</a>-based 3rd District, represented by five-term Democrat <a href="/wiki/Richard_Ray" title="Richard Ray">Richard Ray</a>. Gingrich remarked that "The Speaker, by raising money and gerrymandering, has sincerely dedicated a part of his career to wiping me out."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the same time, the Assembly created a new, heavily Republican 6th District in <a href="/wiki/Fulton_County,_Georgia" title="Fulton County, Georgia">Fulton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cobb_County,_Georgia" title="Cobb County, Georgia">Cobb</a> counties in the wealthy northern suburbs of Atlanta—-an area that Gingrich had never represented. Gingrich sold his home in Carrollton and moved to <a href="/wiki/Marietta,_Georgia" title="Marietta, Georgia">Marietta</a> in the new 6th. His primary opponent, State Representative Herman Clark, made an issue out of Gingrich's 22 kited checks in the House Bank Scandal, and also criticized Gingrich for moving into the district. After a recount, Gingrich prevailed by 980 votes, with a 51% to 49% result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> His winning the primary all but assured him of election in November. He was re-elected three times from this district against nominal Democratic opposition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Republican_Revolution.22_of_1994">"Republican Revolution" of 1994</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title='Edit section: "Republican Revolution" of 1994'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Republican_Revolution" title="Republican Revolution">Republican Revolution</a></div>
<p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives_election,_1994" title="United States House of Representatives election, 1994">1994 campaign season</a>, in an effort to offer an alternative to Democratic policies and to unite distant wings of the Republican Party, Gingrich and several other Republicans came up with a <a href="/wiki/Contract_with_America" title="Contract with America">Contract with America</a>, which laid out ten policies that Republicans promised to bring to a vote on the House floor during the first hundred days of the new Congress, if they won the election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The contract was signed by Gingrich and other Republican candidates for the House of Representatives. The contract ranged from issues such as <a href="/wiki/Welfare_reform" title="Welfare reform">welfare reform</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Term_limits" title="Term limits">term limits</a>, tougher crime laws, and a <a href="/wiki/Balanced_budget" title="Balanced budget">balanced budget</a> law, to more specialized legislation such as restrictions on American military participation in United Nations missions.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In the November 1994 elections, Republicans gained 54 seats and took control of the House for the first time since 1954. Long-time <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_Minority_Leader" title="House Minority Leader">House Minority Leader</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bob_Michel" title="Bob Michel">Bob Michel</a> of <a href="/wiki/Illinois" title="Illinois">Illinois</a> had not run for re-election, giving Gingrich, the highest-ranking Republican returning to Congress, the inside track at becoming Speaker. The midterm election that turned congressional power over to Republicans "changed the center of gravity" in the nation's capital.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> <i>Time</i> magazine named Gingrich its 1995 "Man of the Year" for his role in the election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-npr20111208_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-npr20111208-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Speaker_of_the_House">Speaker of the House</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Speaker of the House">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Contract_with_America" title="Contract with America">Contract with America</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SpeakerGingrich.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="400" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/SpeakerGingrich.jpg/170px-SpeakerGingrich.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/SpeakerGingrich.jpg/255px-SpeakerGingrich.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/SpeakerGingrich.jpg/340px-SpeakerGingrich.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SpeakerGingrich.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gingrich's official portrait as Speaker</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The House fulfilled Gingrich's promise to bring all ten of the Contract's issues to a vote within the first 100 days of the session, even though most of the legislation was initially held up in the Senate by the objection of liberal/progressive interest groups<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> and President Clinton, who called it the "Contract on America".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Legislation proposed by the <a href="/wiki/104th_United_States_Congress" title="104th United States Congress">104th United States Congress</a> included term limits for Congressional Representatives, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tax_cuts" title="Tax cuts">tax cuts</a>, welfare reform, and a <a href="/wiki/Balanced_budget_amendment" title="Balanced budget amendment">balanced budget amendment</a>, as well as independent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auditing" title="Auditing">auditing</a> of the finances of the House of Representatives and elimination of non-essential services such as the House barbershop and shoe-shine concessions. Following Gingrich's first two years as House Speaker, the Republican majority was re-elected in the 1996 election, the first time Republicans had done so in 68 years, and the first time simultaneously with a Democratic president winning re-election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legislation">Legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Welfare_reform">Welfare reform</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Welfare reform">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A central pledge of President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>'s campaign was to reform the welfare system, adding changes such as work requirements for recipients. However, by 1994, the Clinton Administration appeared to be more concerned with pursuing a <a href="/wiki/Universal_health_care" title="Universal health care">universal health care</a> program. Gingrich accused Clinton of stalling on welfare, and proclaimed that Congress could pass a welfare reform bill in as little as 90 days. He insisted that the Republican Party would continue to apply political pressure to the President to approve their welfare legislation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1996, after constructing two welfare reform bills that Clinton vetoed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gillon_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gillon-49">[49]</a></sup> Gingrich and his supporters pushed for passage of the <a href="/wiki/Personal_Responsibility_and_Work_Opportunity_Act" title="Personal Responsibility and Work Opportunity Act">Personal Responsibility and Work Opportunity Act</a>, which was intended to reconstruct the welfare system. The act gave state governments more autonomy over welfare delivery, while also reducing the federal government's responsibilities. It instituted the <a href="/wiki/Temporary_Assistance_for_Needy_Families" title="Temporary Assistance for Needy Families">Temporary Assistance for Needy Families</a> program, which placed time limits on welfare assistance and replaced the longstanding <a href="/wiki/Aid_to_Families_with_Dependent_Children" title="Aid to Families with Dependent Children">Aid to Families with Dependent Children</a> program. Other changes to the welfare system included stricter conditions for food stamp eligibility, reductions in immigrant welfare assistance, and work requirements for recipients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> The bill was signed into law by President Clinton on August 22, 1996.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In his 1998 book <i>Lessons Learned the Hard Way</i>, Gingrich encouraged volunteerism and spiritual renewal, placing more importance on families, creating tax incentives and reducing regulations for businesses in poor neighborhoods, and increasing property ownership by low-income families. He also praised <a href="/wiki/Habitat_for_Humanity" title="Habitat for Humanity">Habitat for Humanity</a> for sparking the movement to improve people's lives by helping them build their own homes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Balancing_the_federal_budget">Balancing the federal budget</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Balancing the federal budget">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Although congressional Republicans had opposed Clinton's <a href="/wiki/Omnibus_Budget_Reconciliation_Act_of_1993" title="Omnibus Budget Reconciliation Act of 1993">Deficit Reduction Act of 1993</a>, a key aspect of the 1994 <a href="/wiki/Contract_with_America" title="Contract with America">Contract with America</a> was the promise of a <a href="/wiki/United_States_federal_budget" title="United States federal budget">balanced federal budget</a>. After the end of the government shutdown, Gingrich and other Republican leaders acknowledged that Congress would not be able to draft a balanced budget in 1996. Instead, they opted to approve some small reductions that were already approved by the White House and to wait until the next election season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>By May 1997, Republican congressional leaders reached a compromise with Democrats and President Clinton on the federal budget. The agreement called for a federal spending plan designed to reduce the federal deficit and achieve a balanced budget by 2002. The plan included a total of $152 billion in bipartisan tax cuts over five years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxcuts_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-taxcuts-53">[53]</a></sup> Other major parts of the spending plan called for $115 billion to be saved through a restructuring of Medicare, $24 billion set aside to extend health insurance to children of the <a href="/wiki/Working_poor" title="Working poor">working poor</a>, tax credits for college tuition, and a $2 billion welfare-to-work jobs initiative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>President Clinton signed the budget legislation in August 1997. At the signing, Gingrich gave credit to ordinary Americans stating, "It was their political will that brought the two parties together."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-taxcuts_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-taxcuts-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early 1998, with the economy performing better than expected, increased tax revenues helped reduce the federal budget deficit to below $25 billion. Clinton submitted a balanced budget for 1999, three years ahead of schedule originally proposed, making it the first time the federal budget had been balanced since 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WashTimes1998_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-WashTimes1998-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxpayer_Relief_Act_of_1997">Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In 1997 President Clinton signed into effect the <a href="/wiki/Taxpayer_Relief_Act_of_1997" title="Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997">Taxpayer Relief Act of 1997</a>, which included the largest <a href="/wiki/Capital_gains_tax_in_the_United_States" title="Capital gains tax in the United States">capital gains tax</a> cut in U.S. history. Under the act, the profits on the sale of a personal residence ($500,000 for married couples, $250,000 for singles) were exempted if lived in for at least 2 years over the last 5. (This had previously been limited to a $125,000 once-in-a-lifetime exemption for those over the age of 55.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> There were also reductions in a number of other taxes on investment gains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FindLaw_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FindLaw-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, the act raised the value of inherited <a href="/wiki/Estate_(law)" title="Estate (law)">estates</a> and gifts that could be sheltered from taxation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FindLaw_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-FindLaw-59">[59]</a></sup> Gingrich has been credited with creating the agenda for the reduction in capital gains tax, especially in the "Contract with America", which set out to balance the budget and implement decreases in estate and capital gains tax. Some Republicans felt that the compromise reached with Clinton on the budget and tax act was inadequate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> however Gingrich has stated that the tax cuts were a significant accomplishment for the Republican Congress in the face of opposition from the Clinton administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Gingrich along with <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dole" title="Bob Dole">Bob Dole</a> had earlier set-up the <a href="/wiki/Kemp_Commission" title="Kemp Commission">Kemp Commission</a>, headed by former US Secretary of Housing and Urban Development <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kemp" title="Jack Kemp">Jack Kemp</a>, a tax reform commission that made several recommendations including that dividends, interest, and capital gains should be untaxed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-two_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-two-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_legislation">Other legislation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other legislation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Among the first pieces of legislation passed by the new Congress under Gingrich was the <a href="/wiki/Congressional_Accountability_Act_of_1995" title="Congressional Accountability Act of 1995">Congressional Accountability Act of 1995</a>, which subjected members of Congress to the same laws that apply to businesses and their employees, including the <a href="/wiki/Civil_Rights_Act_of_1964" title="Civil Rights Act of 1964">Civil Rights Act of 1964</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Americans_with_Disabilities_Act_of_1990" title="Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990">Americans with Disabilities Act of 1990</a>. As a provision of the Contract with America, the law was symbolic of the new Republican majority's goal to remove some of the entitlements enjoyed by Congress. The bill received near universal acceptance from the House and Senate and was signed into law on January 23, 1995.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gingrich shut down the highly regarded <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Technology_Assessment" title="Office of Technology Assessment">Office of Technology Assessment</a>, and relied instead on what the Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists called "self-interested lobbyists and think tanks".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_shutdown">Government shutdown</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Government shutdown">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/United_States_federal_government_shutdown_of_1995_and_1996" title="United States federal government shutdown of 1995 and 1996">United States federal government shutdown of 1995 and 1996</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Nydailynews_newt.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="249" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/87/Nydailynews_newt.jpg/170px-Nydailynews_newt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/87/Nydailynews_newt.jpg 1.5x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Nydailynews_newt.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daily_News_(New_York)" title="Daily News (New York)">Daily News</a></i> cover with editorial cartoon by <a href="/wiki/Ed_Murawinski" title="Ed Murawinski">Ed Murawinski</a> depicting Gingrich as an infant throwing a tantrum</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gingrich and the incoming Republican majority's promise to slow the rate of government spending conflicted with the president's agenda for <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(United_States)" title="Medicare (United States)">Medicare</a>, education, the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a> and <a href="/wiki/Public_health" title="Public health">public health</a>, leading to two temporary shutdowns of the federal government totaling 28 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fram_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-fram-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>Clinton said Republican amendments would strip the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">U.S. Treasury</a> of its ability to dip into federal trust funds to avoid a borrowing crisis. Republican amendments would have limited appeals by death-row inmates, made it harder to issue health, safety and environmental regulations, and would have committed the president to a seven-year balanced budget. Clinton vetoed a second bill allowing the government to keep operating beyond the time when most spending authority expires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fram_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-fram-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>A GOP amendment opposed by Clinton would have not only have increased <a href="/wiki/Medicare_(United_States)#Part_B:_Medical_insurance" title="Medicare (United States)">Medicare Part B</a> premiums, but it would also cancel a scheduled reduction. The Republicans held out for an increase in Medicare part B premiums in January 1996 to $53.50 a month. Clinton favored the then current law, which was to let the premium that seniors pay drop to $42.50.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fram_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-fram-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>The government closed most non-essential offices during the shutdown, which was the longest in U.S. history. The shutdown ended when Clinton agreed to submit a CBO-approved balanced budget plan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the crisis, Gingrich's public image suffered from the perception that the Republicans' hardline budget stance was owed partly to an alleged snub of Gingrich by Clinton during a flight on Air Force One to and from <a href="/wiki/Yitzhak_Rabin" title="Yitzhak Rabin">Yitzhak Rabin</a>'s funeral in Israel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> That perception developed after the trip when Gingrich, while being questioned by <a href="/wiki/Lars-Erik_Nelson" title="Lars-Erik Nelson">Lars-Erik Nelson</a> at a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_Science_Monitor" title="Christian Science Monitor">Christian Science Monitor</a></i> breakfast, said that he was dissatisfied that Clinton had not invited him to discuss the budget during the flight.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-columbia_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-columbia-69">[69]</a></sup> He complained that he and Dole were instructed to use the plane's rear exit to deplane, saying the snub was "part of why you ended up with us sending down a tougher continuing resolution".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> In response to Gingrich's complaint that they were "forced to use the rear door," NBC news released their videotape footage showing both Gingrich and Dole disembarking at Tel Aviv just behind Clinton via the front stairway.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seattle-Times-111695_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seattle-Times-111695-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gingrich was widely lampooned for implying that the government shutdown was a result of his personal grievances, including a widely shared editorial cartoon depicting him as a baby throwing a <a href="/wiki/Tantrum" title="Tantrum">tantrum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Democratic leaders, including <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Schumer" title="Chuck Schumer">Chuck Schumer</a>, took the opportunity to attack Gingrich's motives for the budget standoff.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> In 1998, Gingrich said that these comments were his "single most avoidable mistake" as Speaker.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Discussing the impact of the government shutdown on the Republican Party, Gingrich later commented that, "Everybody in Washington thinks that was a big mistake. They're exactly wrong. There had been no reelected Republican majority since 1928. Part of the reason we got reelected ... is our base thought we were serious. And they thought we were serious because when it came to a show-down, we didn't flinch."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> In a 2011 op-ed in <i>The Washington Post</i>, Gingrich said that the government shutdown led to the balanced-budget deal in 1997 and the first four consecutive balanced budgets since the 1920s, as well as the first re-election of a Republican majority since 1928.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gingrich_WaPo_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gingrich_WaPo-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics_charges_and_reprimand">Ethics charges and reprimand</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ethics charges and reprimand">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clinton1997SOTU.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="577" data-file-width="447" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Clinton1997SOTU.jpg/170px-Clinton1997SOTU.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Clinton1997SOTU.jpg/255px-Clinton1997SOTU.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Clinton1997SOTU.jpg/340px-Clinton1997SOTU.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clinton1997SOTU.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Vice President <a href="/wiki/Al_Gore" title="Al Gore">Al Gore</a>, House Speaker Newt Gingrich and President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a> at the 1997 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_Address" title="State of the Union Address">State of the Union Address</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Eighty-four ethics charges were filed by Democrats against Gingrich during his term as Speaker. All were eventually dropped except for one: claiming tax-exempt status for a college course run for political purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-curtawapo_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-curtawapo-78">[78]</a></sup> On January 21, 1997, the House officially <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_Representatives_expelled,_censured,_or_reprimanded#Reprimanded_Representatives" title="List of United States Representatives expelled, censured, or reprimanded">reprimanded</a> Gingrich (in a vote of 395 in favor, 28 opposed) and "ordered [him] to reimburse the House for some of the costs of the investigation in the amount of $300,000".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HSCE_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-HSCE-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yang012297_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-yang012297-81">[81]</a></sup> It was the first time a Speaker was disciplined for an ethics violation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yang012297_81-1"><a href="#cite_note-yang012297-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clymer012297_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-clymer012297-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>Additionally, the House Ethics Committee concluded that inaccurate information supplied to investigators represented "intentional or ... reckless" disregard of House rules.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dewar_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-dewar-83">[83]</a></sup> The Ethics Committee's Special Counsel <a href="/wiki/James_M._Cole" title="James M. Cole">James M. Cole</a> concluded that Gingrich had violated federal tax law and had lied to the ethics panel in an effort to force the committee to dismiss the complaint against him. The full committee panel did not agree whether tax law had been violated and left that issue up to the <a href="/wiki/Internal_Revenue_Service" title="Internal Revenue Service">IRS</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dewar_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-dewar-83">[83]</a></sup> In 1999, the IRS cleared the organizations connected with the "Renewing American Civilization" courses under investigation for possible tax violations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<p>Regarding the situation, Gingrich said in January 1997, "I did not manage the effort intensely enough to thoroughly direct or review information being submitted to the committee on my behalf. In my name and over my signature, inaccurate, incomplete and unreliable statements were given to the committee, but I did not intend to mislead the committee ... I brought down on the people's house a controversy which could weaken the faith people have in their government."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-clymer122296_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-clymer122296-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leadership_challenge">Leadership challenge</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Leadership challenge">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the summer of 1997 several House Republicans attempted to replace him as Speaker, claiming Gingrich's public image was a liability. The attempted "coup" began July 9 with a meeting of Republican conference chairman <a href="/wiki/John_Boehner" title="John Boehner">John Boehner</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ohio" title="Ohio">Ohio</a> and Republican leadership chairman <a href="/wiki/Bill_Paxon" title="Bill Paxon">Bill Paxon</a> of New York. According to their plan, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_Majority_Leader" title="House Majority Leader">House Majority Leader</a> <a href="/wiki/Dick_Armey" title="Dick Armey">Dick Armey</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_Majority_Whip" title="House Majority Whip">House Majority Whip</a> <a href="/wiki/Tom_DeLay" title="Tom DeLay">Tom DeLay</a>, Boehner and Paxon were to present Gingrich with an ultimatum: resign, or be voted out. However, Armey balked at the proposal to make Paxon the new Speaker, and told his chief of staff to warn Gingrich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> On July 11, Gingrich met with senior Republican leadership to assess the situation. He explained that under no circumstance would he step down. If he was voted out, there would be a new election for Speaker. This would allow for the possibility that Democrats, along with dissenting Republicans, would vote in Democrat <a href="/wiki/Dick_Gephardt" title="Dick Gephardt">Dick Gephardt</a> as Speaker. On July 16, Paxon offered to resign his post, feeling that he had not handled the situation correctly, as the only member of the leadership who had been appointed to his position—by Gingrich—instead of elected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resignation">Resignation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Resignation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1998, Republicans lost five seats in the House—the worst midterm performance in 64 years by a party not holding the presidency. Gingrich, who won his reelection, was held largely responsible for Republican losses in the House. His private polls had given his fellow Republican Congressmen a false impression that pushing the <a href="/wiki/Lewinsky_scandal" title="Lewinsky scandal">Lewinsky scandal</a> would damage Clinton's popularity and result in the party winning a net total of six to thirty seats in the US House of Representatives in this election.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kc.l_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-kc.l-88">[88]</a></sup> The day after the election, a Republican caucus ready to rebel against him prompted his resignation of the speakership. He also announced his intended and eventual full departure from the House in January 1999.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1998_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1998-89">[89]</a></sup> When relinquishing the speakership, Gingrich said he was "not willing to preside over people who are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannibal" title="Cannibal">cannibals</a>," and claimed that leaving the House would keep him from overshadowing his successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1998_89-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1998-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-speakership">Post-speakership</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Post-speakership">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Gingrich has since remained involved in national politics and public policy debate, especially on issues regarding healthcare, national security, and the role of religion in American public life.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy">Policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Former_Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_Newt_Gingrich_speaking_at_the_Republican_Leadership_Conference_in_New_Orleans,_Louisiana.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2840" data-file-width="4170" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Former_Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_Newt_Gingrich_speaking_at_the_Republican_Leadership_Conference_in_New_Orleans%2C_Louisiana.jpg/220px-Former_Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_Newt_Gingrich_speaking_at_the_Republican_Leadership_Conference_in_New_Orleans%2C_Louisiana.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Former_Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_Newt_Gingrich_speaking_at_the_Republican_Leadership_Conference_in_New_Orleans%2C_Louisiana.jpg/330px-Former_Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_Newt_Gingrich_speaking_at_the_Republican_Leadership_Conference_in_New_Orleans%2C_Louisiana.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Former_Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_Newt_Gingrich_speaking_at_the_Republican_Leadership_Conference_in_New_Orleans%2C_Louisiana.jpg/440px-Former_Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_Newt_Gingrich_speaking_at_the_Republican_Leadership_Conference_in_New_Orleans%2C_Louisiana.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Former_Speaker_of_the_U.S._House_Newt_Gingrich_speaking_at_the_Republican_Leadership_Conference_in_New_Orleans,_Louisiana.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gingrich speaking at the 2011 <a href="/wiki/Republican_Leadership_Conference" title="Republican Leadership Conference">Republican Leadership Conference</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" title="New Orleans, Louisiana">New Orleans, Louisiana</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 2003 he founded the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Health_Transformation" title="Center for Health Transformation">Center for Health Transformation</a> to develop a 21st-century healthcare system that is centered on the individual, prevention focused, knowledge intense, and innovation rich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Gingrich supported the <a href="/wiki/Medicare_Prescription_Drug,_Improvement,_and_Modernization_Act" title="Medicare Prescription Drug, Improvement, and Modernization Act">Medicare Prescription Drug, Improvement, and Modernization Act</a> of 2003, creating the <a href="/wiki/Medicare_Part_D" title="Medicare Part D">Medicare Part D</a> federal prescription drugs benefit program. Some conservatives have criticized him for favoring the plan, due to its cost. However, Gingrich has remained a supporter, stating in a 2011 interview that it was a necessary modernization of Medicare, which was created before pharmaceutical drugs became standard in medical care. He has said that the increase in cost from medication must be seen as preventive, leading to reduced need for medical procedures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> In a May 15, 2011, interview on Meet the Press, Gingrich repeated his long-held belief that "all of us have a responsibility to pay—help pay for health care", and suggested this could be implemented by either a mandate to obtain health insurance or a requirement to post a bond ensuring coverage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo20110516_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo20110516-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PoliticoMarr20110516_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-PoliticoMarr20110516-93">[93]</a></sup> In the same interview Gingrich said "I don't think right wing social engineering is any more desirable than left wing social engineering. I don't think imposing radical change from the right or the left is a very good way for a free society to operate." This comment caused back-lash within the Republican Party.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WaPo20110516_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-WaPo20110516-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PoliticoMarr20110516_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-PoliticoMarr20110516-93">[93]</a></sup> Gingrich has also been an advocate for health information technology.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 2005, with <a href="/wiki/Hillary_Clinton" title="Hillary Clinton">Hillary Clinton</a>, Gingrich announced the proposed <i>21st Century Health Information Act</i>, a bill which aimed to replace paperwork with confidential, electronic health information networks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Gingrich also co-chaired an independent congressional study group made up of health policy experts formed in 2007 to evaluate the strengths and weaknesses of action taken within the U.S. to fight <a href="/wiki/Alzheimer%27s_disease" title="Alzheimer's disease">Alzheimer's disease</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gingrich has served on several commissions, including the Hart-Rudman Commission, formally known as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/US_Commission_on_National_Security/21st_Century" title="US Commission on National Security/21st Century">U.S. Commission on National Security/21st century</a>, which examined issues affecting the armed forces, law enforcement and intelligence agencies with regards to national security.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> In 2005 he became the co-chair of a task force for UN reform, which aimed to produce a plan for the U.S. to help strengthen the UN.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> For over two decades, Gingrich has taught at the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Air_Force" title="United States Air Force">United States Air Force</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Air_University_(United_States_Air_Force)" title="Air University (United States Air Force)">Air University</a>, where he is the longest-serving teacher of the Joint Flag Officer Warfighting Course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> In addition, he is an honorary Distinguished Visiting Scholar and Professor at the <a href="/wiki/National_Defense_University" title="National Defense University">National Defense University</a> and teaches officers from all of the defense services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Gingrich informally advised Defense secretary <a href="/wiki/Donald_Rumsfeld" title="Donald Rumsfeld">Donald Rumsfeld</a> on strategic issues, on issues including the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a> and encouraging the Pentagon to not "yield" foreign policy influence to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/State_Department" title="State Department">State Department</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_National_Security_Council" title="United States National Security Council">National Security Council</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Gingrich is also a guiding coalition member of the <a href="/wiki/Project_on_National_Security_Reform" title="Project on National Security Reform">Project on National Security Reform</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In September 2007, Gingrich founded the 527 group <a href="/wiki/American_Solutions_for_Winning_the_Future" title="American Solutions for Winning the Future">American Solutions for Winning the Future</a>. The stated mission of the group is to become the "leading grassroots movement to recruit, educate, and empower citizen activists and elected officials to develop solutions to transform all levels of government". Gingrich spoke of the group and its objectives at the CPAC conference of 2008 and currently serves as its General Chairman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Other organizations and companies founded or chaired by Gingrich include the creative production company Gingrich Productions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> and religious educational organization Renewing American Leadership.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>Gingrich is a member of the <a href="/wiki/Council_on_Foreign_Relations" title="Council on Foreign Relations">Council on Foreign Relations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></p>
<p>He is a fellow at conservative <a href="/wiki/Think_tank" title="Think tank">think tanks</a> the <a href="/wiki/American_Enterprise_Institute" title="American Enterprise Institute">American Enterprise Institute</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hoover_Institution" title="Hoover Institution">Hoover Institution</a>, focusing on U.S. politics, world history, national security policy, and environmental policy issues. He sometimes serves as a commentator, guest or panel member on <a href="/wiki/United_States_cable_news" title="United States cable news">cable news</a> shows, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fox_News_Channel" title="Fox News Channel">Fox News Channel</a>. He is listed as a contributor by Fox News Channel, and frequently appears as a guest on various segments; he has also hosted occasional specials for the Fox News Channel. Gingrich is a proponent of the <a href="/wiki/Six_Sigma" title="Six Sigma">Lean Six Sigma</a> management techniques for waste reduction,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> and has signed the "Strong America Now" pledge committing to promoting the methods to reduce government spending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Businesses">Businesses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Businesses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After leaving Congress in 1999, Gingrich started a number of for-profit companies:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-house_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-house-108">[108]</a></sup> Between 2001 and 2010, the companies he and his wife owned in full or part had revenues of almost $100 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inc_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Inc-109">[109]</a></sup> Currently, Gingrich serves as an advisor to the Canadian mining company <a href="/wiki/Barrick_Gold" title="Barrick Gold">Barrick Gold</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to financial disclosure forms released in July 2011, Gingrich and his wife had a net worth of at least $6.7 million in 2010, compared to a maximum net worth of $2.4 million in 2006. Most of the increase in his net worth was because of payments to him from his for-profit companies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-net_worth_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-net_worth-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gingrich_Group_and_the_Center_for_Health_Transformation">Gingrich Group and the Center for Health Transformation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Gingrich Group and the Center for Health Transformation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>The Gingrich Group was organized in 1999 as a consulting company. Over time, its non-health clients were dropped, and it was renamed the <a href="/wiki/Center_for_Health_Transformation" title="Center for Health Transformation">Center for Health Transformation</a>. The two companies had revenues of $55 million between 2001 and 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.2455_million_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-.2455_million-112">[112]</a></sup> The revenues came from more than 300 health-insurance companies and other clients, with membership costing as much as $200,000 per year in exchange for access to Gingrich and other perks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Inc_109-1"><a href="#cite_note-Inc-109">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> In 2011, when Gingrich became a presidential candidate, he sold his interest in the business and said he would release the full list of his clients and the amounts he was paid, "to the extent we can".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.2455_million_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-.2455_million-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-small_business_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-small_business-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2012, the Center for Health Transformation filed for <a href="/wiki/Chapter_7,_Title_11,_United_States_Code" title="Chapter 7, Title 11, United States Code">Chapter 7 bankruptcy</a>, planning to liquidate its assets to meet debts of $1–$10 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-abc-bankruptcy_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-abc-bankruptcy-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt-bankruptcy_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt-bankruptcy-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 2001 and 2010, Gingrich consulted for <a href="/wiki/Freddie_Mac" title="Freddie Mac">Freddie Mac</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Government-sponsored_enterprise" title="Government-sponsored enterprise">government-sponsored</a> secondary home mortgage company, which was concerned about new regulations under consideration by Congress. Regarding payments of $1.6 million for the consulting,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-.2455_million_112-2"><a href="#cite_note-.2455_million-112">[112]</a></sup> Gingrich said that "Freddie Mac paid Gingrich Group, which has a number of employees and a number of offices, a consulting fee, just like you would pay any other consulting firm."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-distances_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-distances-117">[117]</a></sup> In January 2012, he said that he could not make public his contract with Freddie Mac, even though the company gave permission, until his business partners in the Center for Health Transformation also agreed to that.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Gingrich_Productions">Gingrich Productions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Newt_Gingrich&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Gingrich Productions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Gingrich Productions, which is headed by Gingrich's wife <a href="/wiki/Callista_Gingrich" title="Callista Gingrich">Callista Gingrich</a>, was created in 2007. According to the company's website, in May 2011, it is "a performance and production company featuring the work of Newt and Callista Gingrich. Newt and Callista host and produce historical and public policy documentaries, write books, record audio books and voiceovers, produce photographic essays, and make television and radio appearances."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-small_business_114-1"><a href="#cite_note-small_business-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p>Between 2008 and 2011, the company produced three films on religion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeleny226_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeleny226-119">[119]</a></sup> one on energy, one on Ronald Reagan, and one on the threat of radical Islam.
